#### *Geography & Locations*

Hindustani \- refers to an Indian, irrespective of religious affiliation. Among non-Hindustani speakers e.g. Bengali-speakers, "Hindustani" is used to describe persons who are from the upper Ganges, also regardless of religious affiliation, but rather as a geographic term  
Paternoster Row \- a London street that was the centre of the London publishing trade, with booksellers operating from the street, described as “almost synonymous” with the book trade.  
Belgravia \- an affluent district in Central London  
The Savile Club \- established in 1866 by a group of the most distinguished writers and artists of the time, it still operates, out of an 18th-century house in Mayfair  
Vauxhall \- with a long history as a location for entertainment and culture, it’s famous for the Victorian Pleasure Gardens, and is now home to one of London’s foremost LGBTQ+ nightlife centres.  
Bermondsey \- a district in South East London in the London Borough of Southwark, England, 2.5 miles (4.0 km) southeast of Charing Cross.  
**Soho** (bohemian area) \- [Soho Square and its neighbourhood](https://www.british-history.ac.uk/old-new-london/vol3/pp184-196)  
**St. James** \- home to many of the best known gentlemen's clubs in London, and sometimes, though not as often as formerly, referred to as "Clubland"  
Tower of London \- a notorious prison and place of execution   
**Savile Row** \- street in Mayfair, central London, known mostly for its traditional bespoke tailoring for men, it has had a varied history that has included accommodating the headquarters of the Royal Geographical Society (founded 1830), where significant British explorations to Africa & the South Pole were planned.  
Haymarket, London \- Haymarket is a street in the St James's area of the City of Westminster, Central London. It runs from Piccadilly Circus in the north to Pall Mall at the southern end.  
**Pall Mall** \- a street in St James, Westminster. It connects St James's Street to Trafalgar Square and is a section of the regional A4 road. The street's name is derived from 'pall-mall', a ball game played there during the 17th century.  
Piccadilly Circus \- A busy road junction in the West End of London  
Great Portland Street \- In the West End of London: links Oxford Street with Albany Street and the A501 Marylebone Road and Euston Road. A commercial street including some embassies, it divides Fitzrovia, to the east, from Marylebone to the west. It deliniates areas with contrasting identities, the west at strongest in grandiose Portland Place and Harley Street, the east at strongest in artists' and independent businesses of Fitzrovia. The street has its own unique character, due in part of the unusual combination of small shops combined with its strongly rectilinear character.  
Harrogate \- a ‘spa town’ in North Yorkshire, England. [Historically](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Historic_counties_of_England) in the [West Riding of Yorkshire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Riding_of_Yorkshire), the town is a tourist destination and its visitor attractions include its spa waters and [RHS Harlow Carr gardens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harlow_Carr) (Royal Horticultural Society).  
Whitechapel, London \- A vibrant, multicultural district in the East End, with many traditional pubs and curry houses, and the lively Whitechapel Market, a focal point for Asian food, discount fashion, and homewares. The cobblestone alleys here are the setting for guided walks about the 1800s Jack the Ripper murders, while the trendy Whitechapel Gallery hosts contemporary art exhibitions and film screenings.  
Flower and Dean Street \- Flower and Dean Street was a road at the heart of the Spitalfields rookery in the East End of London. It was one of the most notorious slums of the Victorian era, being described in 1883 as “perhaps the foulest and most dangerous street in the whole metropolis,” and was closely associated with the victims of Jack the Ripper.  
Spitalfields \- a district in the East End of London and within the London Borough of Tower Hamlets. The area is formed around Commercial Street (on the A1202 London Inner Ring Road) and includes the locale around Brick Lane, Christ Church, Toynbee Hall and Commercial Tavern. It has several markets, including Spitalfields Market, the historic Old Spitalfields Market, Brick Lane Market and Petticoat Lane Market. It was part of the ancient parish of Stepney in the county of Middlesex and was split off as a separate parish in 1729\. Just outside the City of London, the parish became part of the Metropolitan Board of Works area in 1855 as part of the Whitechapel District.  
The Metropolitan Board of Works (MBW) \- the principal instrument of London-wide government from December 1855 until the establishment of the London County Council in March 1889\. Its principal responsibility was to provide infrastructure to cope with London's rapid growth, which it accomplished. The MBW was **an appointed rather than elected body.** This lack of accountability made it unpopular with Londoners, especially in its latter years when it **fell prey to corruption.**  
The Spitalfields Tenterground \- established in the 17th century by Flemish weavers, who were Huguenot refugees fleeing religious persecution. Their weaving industry led to the area becoming a centre of the garment industry (the rag trade as it became known colloquially), with names such as Fashion Street and Petticoat Lane still extant. It was originally an area of open ground about 150 yards square, surrounded by the weavers' houses and workshops in White's Row, Wentworth Street, Bell Lane and Rose Lane (the last of which no longer exists). By the 19th century, the Flemish weavers had dispersed, and in 1829 the Tenterground was developed for housing. From about 1850, it was populated by Dutch Jews (see Chuts), to be joined later by Jewish refugees fleeing persecution in eastern Europe (see pogrom).  
Tenterground, tenter ground or teneter-field \- an area used for drying newly manufactured cloth after fulling. The wet cloth was hooked onto frames called tenters and stretched taut so that the cloth would dry flat and square. It is from this process that some have the expression "on tenterhooks", meaning in a state of nervous tension. There were tentergrounds wherever cloth was made, and as a result the word "tenter" is found in place names throughout the United Kingdom and its empire, for example several streets in Spitalfields, London and Tenterfield House in Haddington, East Lothian, Scotland, which is turn gave its name to Tenterfield in New South Wales, Australia.  
**Limehouse (Docks)** \- In 1766, the Limehouse Bridge Dock was constructed for barges and small ships to access the ‘Limehouse Cut’, which was a man-made canal that led further inland to the River Lee. Although most of the trading ships cargoes were unloaded further up the Thames at the Pool of London – the ‘Limehouse Basin’ was opened in 1820 as the Regent’s Canal Dock, specifically to transport unloaded smaller goods and cargo, on to barges on the Regent’s Canal – making Limehouse an important connection between the Thames and the canal systems, where cargoes could be transferred from larger ships to the smaller canal boats. Chinese sailors generally traded in tea and opium, and the area became notorious for its opium dens. It was also **famous for its Chinese Laundry's** – and as a result the area developed its own Chinatown district – the first in London.  
Public house, byname pub \- an establishment providing alcoholic beverages to be consumed on the premises. The traditional pub is an establishment found primarily in Britain and regions of British influence.  
West India Docks \- Built into the Isle of Dogs, for ships to dock and unload passengers and merchandise (initially from West India). Construction work of the two original West India Docks was sanctioned in 1799 by Parliament. They were built by the West India Dock Company for trade with the West Indies in tobacco, rice, wine, brandy and rum. Laying of the first stone took place on 12 July 1800\. The Isle of Dogs was at that time mostly open meadowland where cows were grazed, with ship-breakers’ yards, rope-makers and timber merchants along the riverside. The civil engineer William Jessop (also responsible for the Grand Junction Canal from the Midlands to London) was commissioned to create the docks and warehouses. He in turn left much of the work to Ralph Walker, one of those who had originally proposed the docks. The Scottish engineer John Rennie acted as a consultant and he introduced steam power for both the construction work and the dockside cranage.  
East India Trading Co. \- This company rose to account for half of the world's trade during the mid-1700s and early 1800s, particularly in basic commodities including cotton, silk, indigo dye, sugar, salt, spices, saltpetre, tea, and opium. The company also ruled the beginnings of the British Empire in India. The company eventually came to rule large areas of India, exercising military power and assuming administrative functions. Company rule in India effectively began in 1757 after the Battle of Plassey and lasted until 1858 when, following the Indian Rebellion of 1857, the Government of India Act 1858 led to the British Crown assuming direct control of India in the form of the new British Raj. Despite frequent government intervention, the company had recurring problems with its finances. The company was dissolved in 1874 as a result of the East India Stock Dividend Redemption Act enacted one year earlier, as the Government of India Act had by then rendered it vestigial, powerless, and obsolete. The official government machinery of British Raj (the rule of the British Crown on the Indian subcontinent from 1858 to 1947, AKA Crown rule, or direct rule) had assumed its governmental functions and absorbed its armies.  
**Rookery** \- a dense collection of housing, especially in a slum area. "they congregate cheek by jowl in unwholesome rookeries chosen for cheapness and convenience" Also— a colloquial English term given in the 18th and 19th centuries to a city slum occupied by poor people and frequently also by criminals and prostitutes. Such areas were overcrowded, with low-quality housing and little or no sanitation. Poorly constructed dwellings, built with multiple storeys and often crammed into any area of open ground, created densely-populated areas of gloomy, narrow streets and alleyways.  
Rag-and-bone shop \- A dealership selling cheap unwanted second-hand goods, or bric-a-brac, that have been collected for nothing as disposable items.  
Theatre Royal Haymarket (AKA Haymarket Theatre or the Little Theatre) \- West End theatre on Haymarket in the City of Westminster which dates back to 1720, making it the third-oldest London playhouse still in use. Samuel Foote acquired the lease in 1747, and in 1766 he gained a royal patent to play legitimate drama (meaning spoken drama, as opposed to opera, concerts or plays with music) in the summer months.  
‘French Mexico’ \- French immigration to Mexico started after Mexican independence in 1821, as foreign immigration was prohibited by Spanish authorities under the colonial regime. The first wave of French immigration occurred in the 1830s, following the country's recognition by France, with the foundation of a French colony on the Coatzacoalcos River, in Veracruz. 668 settlers were brought from France to populate the colony. Now there’s \~10,000 French Mexicans in the state of Veracruz.  
Sussex \- from the Old English Sūþsēaxe, a historic county in South East England and was formerly an independent mediaeval kingdom.  
Ellynge (Sussex) \- Solitary; far from neighbours; uncanny. “Tis a terrible ellynge lonesome old house”  
Long Acre \- a street in the City of Westminster in central London. It runs from St Martin's Lane, at its western end, to Drury Lane in the east. The street was completed in the early 17th century and was once known for its coach-makers…  
Lambeth \- a riverside area home to Lambeth Palace, the residence of the Archbishop of Canterbury, with its 13th-century chapel.  
Kensington \- With stately Victorian buildings, it’s one of the most affluent and posh areas of central London. A district of West London, it’s within the Royal Borough of Kensington and Chelsea. It’s densely populated and home to the well-known museum district of South Kensington.  
(Satan’s) Pandæmonium \- the capital of Hell in John Milton's epic poem Paradise Lost  
Mizmaze(s) \- Mazes were often around for centuries before being recorded. The Breamore Maze, for example, was first recorded in 1783, but its origins certainly go back centuries before that, built by the inhabitants of the priory (a monastery of men or women under religious vows that is headed by a prior or prioress). The Breamore Mizmaze is thought to date to the 12th or 13th centuries. It was used by the monks for penitential purposes. They would crawl along the path to the central mound, all the while reflecting on their sins and saying prayers at specific points.  
The Underground (our usage) \- Subterranean tunnels of sewer and railway, connecting the Docklands with all of London.  
Romanis \- An ethnic group of traditionally itinerant people originating from northern India

#### *Furniture & Decor*

Gingerbread \- Fancy decoration, especially on a building. "The mahogany legs had been detached from Oliphant's dining-table, and the great polished surface now sat on its corners of carven gingerbread, mere inches above the floor."  
Commode \- 1\. a piece of furniture containing a concealed chamber pot. 2\. a chest of drawers or chiffonier of a decorative type popular in the 18th century.a  
**Chiffonier** \- (American) a tall chest of drawers, often with a mirror on top. (British) a low cupboard, sometimes with a raised bookshelf on top.  
Cheval glass \- a tall mirror fitted at its middle to an upright frame so that it can be tilted.  
Bouillotte lamp \- consisting of one or several candlesticks with a central standard equipped with a non-flammable adjustable shade, often made of tôle, a painted or lacquered metal, reflective white on the inside, dark on the outside, that could be lowered as the candles burned down.  
Bouillotte \- an 18th-century French gambling card game of the Revolution based on Brelan, very popular during the 19th century in France and again for some years from 1830\. It was also popular in America. The game is regarded as one of the games that influenced the open-card stud variation in poker. It also gave rise to the Bouillotte lamp.  
Toleware \- The term tôle, derived from the French tôle peinte, "painted sheet metal", is synonymous in English usage with japanning on tin, such as the tôle shades for bouillotte lamps and other candle shades, and trays and lidded canisters, in which stenciling and gilding often features, almost always on a black ground. Pontypool and Usk in South Wales made a reputation for tôle imitating Japanese lacquer starting in the early 19th century.   
Japanning (such as “japanned tin”) \- a type of finish that originated as a European imitation of Asian lacquer work. It was first used on furniture, but was later much used on small items in metal. The word originated in the 17th century. American work is more often called *toleware*. (See above.) Although often referred to as lacquer, it is distinct from true East Asian lacquer, which is made by coating objects with a preparation based on the dried sap of the Toxicodendron vernicifluum tree, which was not available in Europe.  
Haviland China \- Throughout the 1830's, David Haviland was a partner in D.G. & D. Haviland Trading Co. \[...\] Kaolin, a cream-colored clay capable of creating superior quality porcelain, was discovered in the region of Limoges in 1770\.  
Paste \- hand-cut glass that has been polished with metal powder until it shimmers like a gemstone.  
Paste jewellery became hugely popular in the Georgian era as a cheaper alternative to diamonds.  
Linseed oil \- used in paint and to finish wood floors and other wood-made things, furniture, etc.  
**Filigree** \- A form of intricate metalwork. It may span from delicate jewellery to cast iron railings and balustrades  
Filigreed \- Ornamental design of a delicate, curling nature  
Plank-topped table \-   
Delaminated veneer \-   
**Snug** \- a small room (a snug fit, you might say) with cosy, soft furnishings, where wall panelling and warm colours can be deployed for maximum cosiness and a rustic feel.  
Larder \- a room or large cupboard for storing food.  
(Turkish) Ottoman \- Deeply upholstered seat of any shape, with or without a back, introduced into Europe in the late 18th century from Turkey. “the flooring from wall to wall was **dimpled and buttoned** with thick maroon cushioning, like a Turkish ottoman.”  
Pressed-oak \- Pressed wood, AKA presswood, is any engineered wood made from wood veneers, wood shavings and particles, sawdust or wood fibres bonded together with an adhesive under heat and pressure. The layers are weak on their own, but are stronger when pressed together.  
Trestle table \- an item of furniture consisting of two or three trestle supports linked by a longitudinal cross-member over which a board or tabletop is placed. In the Middle Ages, the trestle table was often little more than loose boards over trestle legs for ease of assembly and storage.  
Salver \- a tray, typically one made of silver and used in formal circumstances.  
Salt cellar \- (also called salt-box and salt pig) tableware for holding/dispensing salt. A British term for the North American English ‘salt shaker’  
Horn lantern \- This lantern has a metal frame and four windows made from animal horn. There are three covered ventilation holes, to let the air in and heat out, and a hinged door. When a light was needed outside candles were carried in a lantern. An old word for lantern was lanthorn, a reference to the horn that formed the panes. Glass was expensive and easily broken. Horn was very tough and long lasting, and while yellow and not clear like glass, it allowed enough light through, by which to see.  
Fretted glass \- fine-crafted glass with frets woven over/through (typically wood or metal) ...not to be confused with: Fritted glass \- finely porous glass through which gas or liquid may pass. It is made by sintering together glass particles into a solid but porous body. This porous glass body can be called a frit. Applications in laboratory glassware include use in fritted glass filter items, scrubbers, or spargers (see entry within Science & Medicine).  
Mullioned \- A vertical element that forms a division between units of a window or screen, or is used decoratively.  
Oakum \- a preparation of tarred fibre used to seal gaps, obtained by untwisting old rope, used especially in caulking wooden ships. Its main traditional applications were in shipbuilding, for caulking or packing the joints of timbers in wooden vessels and the deck planking of iron and steel ships; in plumbing, for sealing joints in cast iron pipe; and in log cabins for chinking.  
Gateleg table \- first developed in 16th century UK, the table top has a fixed section and one of two hinged leaves.

#### *Fashion & Cosmetics*

[Goffered/Goffering](http://www.oldandinteresting.com/fluting-goffering-irons.aspx) \- ‘Anyone who's pressed clothes with an iron on a flat surface knows it's easy to make a crease. So how can you iron waves of ruffles and keep the flounces without flattening them? One solution was a goffering iron, also called an Italian or tally iron. This was like a metal test tube set horizontally on a stand. (See right) The tube was heated by inserting a metal poker-like rod, fresh from the stove or hearth. Then frilled cuffs and collars could be curled round the cylinder, and other trimmings, like ribbons, were moved across it. Some Victorians took pride in a display of expertly-ironed ruffles, and the well-dressed baby often had a bonnet trimmed with "Italian-ironed double frills", as mentioned by Charlotte Brontë in 1849.’ \[...\] ‘For rows of frilled trimming, crimping or goffering (gauffering) tongs could be used. These were applied to starched lace edging or ruffles, once they were arranged ready in a suitable position.’  
Gotten-up \- Raised up, Brought up in: “gotten up in a Frenchified livery” (14)

- [ ] *‘dandies in worsted suits and silk gloves.’*  
      Petticoat \- type of undergarment worn under a skirt or a dress (it takes the shape of a skirt but is more poofy like a tutu), often made of cotton, silk or tulle  
      Tulle \- light, fien, stiff netting. Can be made of various fibres, including silk, nylon, polyester and rayon. Polyester is most common  
      Brogue \- a style of low-heeled shoe/boot traditionally characterised by multiple-piece, sturdy leather uppers with decorative perforations and serration along the pieces’ visible edges.

Knee-breeches \- (typically fall-front)  
Chamois \- a type of soft pliable, porous leather, traditionally the skin of the chamois (Rupicapra rupicapra), a type of European mountain goat, but today made almost exclusively from sheepskin lambskin.  
Motorhuckle boots \- Buckle-y, brutal, and all black leather.  
Patent leather \- leather with a glossy varnished surface. Most patent leather is not actually leather. It used to be made (a century and more ago) by applying layers of oil on top of natural leather. Modern patent leathers are mostly plastic coatings applied over plastic materials, thus making it a plastic material and not actually leather.  
Pince-nez \- eyeglasses with a nose clip instead of earpieces. Marketed as 'sporting pince-nez', they were purportedly more difficult to jar from the face than the other varieties as well as being more comfortable to wear for longer periods.  
Frumpery \- clothing of frumpish (**dowdy** and old-fashioned) appearance; also, frumpy  
Cravat \- Similar to an ascot: a short, wide strip of fabric worn by men around the neck and tucked inside an open-necked shirt, secured with a pin.  
Hobnail \- a short nail with a thick head used to increase the durability of boot soles “hobnailed boots”  
**Petticoat Lane Market** \- a fashion and clothing market in Spitalfields, London.  
Bombazine \- made with a silk warp and a worsted weft. It is twilled or corded and used for dress-material.  
Blocking \- the hand process by which a hat is given its shape. Just as a fine diamond can be enhanced or spoiled by the diamond cutter, a beautiful Montecristi hat body can be enhanced or spoiled by how it is shaped and finished “Having his hat blocked"  
Reblock \- to remould (a hat) into its original shape  
Sleeve garter \- a garter worn on the sleeve of a shirt. It came into wide use, especially in the US, in the latter half of the 19th century when men's ready-made shirts came in a single (extra long) sleeve length. Sleeve garters allow individuals to customise sleeve lengths and keep their cuffs from becoming soiled while working or at the correct length when worn under a jacket.  
Muffler \- a wrap or scarf worn around the neck and face for warmth.  
Cotton duck (from Dutch: doek, "linen canvas") \- AKA duck, duck cloth or duck canvas. It’s a heavy, plain woven cotton fabric, more tightly woven than plain canvas. Used from sneakers to painting canvases to tents to sandbags, there is also linen duck, which is less often used. By treating with wax, duck fabric can be made waterproof (see Waxed Cotton).  
Jute \- name of the plant that burlap is derived from.  
Placket \- an opening or slit in a garment, covering fastenings or giving access to a pocket, or the flap of fabric under such an opening  
Eyelet \- A small hole in leather or cloth for threading a lace or string through   
Punch hole \- a round hole in a belt-strap, which the prong is fed through  
Raiment \- a garment  
Mantelet \- women’s short, loose sleeveless cloak/shawl.  
Twilled \- fabric that's woven so it has a surface of diagonal parallel ridges.  
Worsted  \- a fine smooth yarn spun from combed long-staple wool, or a fabric made from worsted yarn, having a close-textured surface with no nap, as in, "a worsted suit."  
Kid gloves \- made from the pelt of a baby goat  
Braided cap \- Like a smoking/lounging cap \- Like worn by a bellboy  
Derby hat \- a felt hat that is round and hard with a narrow brim. AKA bowler, bowler hat, derby, plug hat.  
Boutonniere \- a spray of flowers worn in a buttonhole.  
Modiste \- (dated) a fashionable milliner or dressmaker.  
Milliner \- a person who makes or sells women's hats.  
Finery \- clothing or decoration (typically expensive or ostentatious)  
Shooting coat \- 1\. a padded waterproof coat with large pockets, worn when shooting game. 2\. (archaic) a morning coat.  
(Prince) Albert coat: a double-breasted frock coat usually with a flat velvet collar and peaked lapels. It buttoned down to the waist seam, which was decorated at the back with a pair of buttons.  
Furbelow \- a gathered strip or pleated border of a skirt or petticoat. Or- V. (literary) to adorn with trimmings.  
String tie \- a very narrow tie, usually tied in a bow (see Ladsman)  
Knickers \- short, women’s underpants  
Waistbelt \- a belt  
Epaulets \- an ornamental shoulder-piece used as an insignia of rank mostly by armed forces  
**Surplice** \- a liturgical vestment of the Western Christian Church. The surplice is in the form of a tunic of white linen or cotton fabric, reaching to the knees, with wide or moderately wide sleeves. It was originally a long garment with open sleeves reaching nearly to the ground.  
Frock \- (British) A woman's or girl's dress.  
Frock coat \- formal men's coat characterised by a knee-length skirt cut all around the base just above the knee. Although black trousers did occur, especially at daytime, the black frock coat was commonly worn with charcoal grey, pin-striped or checked formal trousers.  
Toggle-coat \- (also duffle coat) is a coat made from duffel, a coarse, thick, woolen material. The name derives from Duffel, a town in the province of Antwerp in Belgium where the fabric originated. Duffel bags were originally made from the same material.  
Fulling \[AKA tucking/walking (Scots: waukin, hence often spelled waulking in Scottish English)\] \- A step in woollen cloth making which involves the cleansing of cloth (particularly wool) to eliminate oils, dirt, and other impurities, and to make it thicker. The practice died out with the modernisation of the industrial revolution.  
Robe-de-nuit \- nightgown   
**Braces** \- suspenders  
Unmentionables \- underwear; anything too shocking to be mentioned by name  
Rucked up \- mussed up (of clothing)  
**Orange-water** \- AKA orange blossom water \- the clear, aromatic by-product of the distillation of fresh bitter-orange blossoms for their essential oil (another Victorian one was honeysuckle)  
Perfume \- [Animal-derived](https://www.sylvaine-delacourte.com/en/guide/the-civet#:~:text=The%20smell%20of%20civet%20is,solvent%20and%20infused%20in%20alcohol.), [General History](https://www.mimimatthews.com/2018/03/12/how-much-scent-is-too-much-victorian-advice-for-ladies-and-gentlemen/), [Addtl. History](https://perfumesociety.org/history/the-victorians-from-violet-posies-to-va-va-voom/)  
Arsenic dresses and other [killer clothing](https://www.grunge.com/706880/the-arsenic-dress-and-other-clothing-that-killed-people/) \- [Link 1](http://kvadratinterwoven.com/emerald-green), “This dress\! This emerald green shimmering silk\! The rich colour that withstands even the gaslight of the opera, without drifting into blue\!”

#### *Liquor & Vices*

Gin craze (1720-1760) \- an era whose records illuminate a troubling addiction to a moonshine-like incarnation of the spirit, then distilled in unlicensed backroom bars and served by the dram (in pints). \[...\] During the 1740s an average of over 23 litres of gin was annually consumed per person: today that number is under 10 litres, inclusive of all spirits. The authorities clamped down hard on the gin trade in the mid-18th century, slowly weaning the populace off the harsh grain alcohol. In their absence, pubs had taken over London’s libation landscape, but during the early 19th-century gin shops ([gin palaces](http://natgeotraveller.in/londons-grandest-victorian-gin-palaces/)) re-entered the scene, incentivised by better tax breaks than alehouses.   
Loggerhead \- “Consisting of a long, thick piece of iron whittled down to a handle, loggerheads were placed in a wood-burning fireplace until red-hot and then used to craft punches, toddies, and flips in particular (in the days prior to central heating, [hot serves](https://sipsmith.com/history-hot-gin-cocktails/) were an understandable fixation); and Gin and gingerbread was the preferred refreshment at the Thames Frost Fairs.” \[They likely were out of use by the 1800s, due to the fire hazard, but we can take liberties\]  
**Sporting-men** \- Sporting man culture has little to do with playing sports. Instead it pertains to hedonistic lifestyles such as keeping mistresses, excessive eating, drinking, smoking, gambling (on rat-baiting for example), and big game hunting, and historically applied to a good sized group of middle- and upper-class men in the mid-19th century, most often in the U.K. and U.S.  
Opium \- Narcotic drug obtained from the unripe seedpods of the opium poppy, papaver somniferum  
Hogshead / Hog’s head \- a large cask of liquid, more specifically, it refers to a specified volume, measured in either imperial or US customary measures, primarily applied to alcoholic beverages, such as wine, ale, or cider  
Beer-cask \- a barrel-shape container used for the production, storage, and service of cask-conditioned beer, widely called “real ale” in the UK. The shape of the cask, longer than it is wide and bulging along its length, evolved more than 2,000 years ago for ease of its manufacture out of wood.  
Dipsomaniacal \- characterised by an uncontrollable urge to consume alcohol  
Amontillado \- a Spanish variety of sherry wine characterised by being darker than fino but lighter than oloroso  
Cream sherry \- the general name for different kinds of sweetened sherries. They are usually produced by blending a dry wine like Oloroso with naturally sweet Pedro Ximénez or Moscatel wines.  
Dogman (or dogsman) \- a person who raises dogs for dog fighting (dogsmen)  
Tobacconist \- a dealer in cigarettes, tobacco, cigars, and other items used by smokers.  
Gasper \- (contemporary, at least after 1890\) a cigarette, typically harsh  
Calabash pipe \- a pipe with a curved stem and a bowl made from a calabash gourd  
Briar (or brier) pipe  \- made from root nodules borne at ground level by a large woody plant of the heath family. Beginning as a Burl (or growth) on the root system of the White Heath Tree (a squat, hearty, shrub-like plant which grows primarily in the dry, arid, rocky wastelands around the Mediterranean Sea), of all woods, the Briar Burl is unique for making pipe.  
Gin-twist \- dry (unsweetened) gin, lemon juice (freshly squeezed), sugar syrup  
(Bittered) gin-sling \- (early cocktail — around 1800\) an iced drink made from gin and water, sweetened, and flavoured with lemon or lime juice  
Grog \- any of a variety of alcoholic beverages, originally referring to rum diluted with water (and later on long sea voyages, with the added juice of limes/lemons), which British Vice-Admiral Edward Vernon introduced into the naval squadron he commanded in the West Indies on 21 August 1740\.  
Genever, Jenever: Dutch spirit, still immensely popular in the Netherlands today. Distilled from malt wine and flavoured with juniper, *hence the name jenever*. Also known as **Madam Geneva** in English; Hollands, genever, genièvre, peket, or sometimes Dutch gin  
Old Tom Gin: Now used to refer to a style of gin popular in England in the 19th Century. Typically sweeter than modern gin. Various explanations for how the name came to be. Traditionally often featuring some sort of cat on the bottle.  
London Dry Gin: Modern style of gin, which has dominated since the late 19th Century.  
Plymouth Gin: Similar to London dry gin, although said to be slightly sweeter, and the subject of protected geographical indication status, meaning it can only be made in Plymouth.  
Sloe Gin: A liqueur made from gin and sloe berries from the blackthorn.  
Hucklebuff \- “beer, egg, and brandy, made hot;"   
Huckle \- a dialect word for a stook of corn (wheat), as well as the hip. Buff means naked.  
Gins are usually finished in barrels for a few months before they're ready.  
Hartshorn \- Baker’s ammonia, a leavening ingredient used in old fashioned recipes (typically for extra-crisp crackers, but also to dilute/stretch bar-served liquor) Also— the antler of a male deer.  
Potash \- various mined/manufactured salts containing potassium in water-soluble sorm. Name derives from pot ash, referring to plant ashes or wood ash soaked in water in a pot, which was the primary means of manufacturing the product before the Industrial Era.  
Quassia (wood) \- bark… a flavouring in fluid or powdered extract. No smell, bitter taste. Used in cocktail bitters. “Bitters are to cocktails as salt is to food”  
Noggin \- a small quantity of liquor, typically a quarter of a pint.  
Lees \- 1\. The sediment of wine in the barrel. 2\. The most worthless part or parts of something.  
Honey gin/the Bee’s Knees cocktail  
Shickered \- drunk (US, informal, first used in the late 19th century)  
Squiffy \- slightly drunk or tipsy  
Chateaued \- To be drunk and retain a classy demeanour: especially when one is drinking expensive wine.  
Pottery canteen \- ceramic canteen (with a strap or a chain for carrying)  
Inebriant \- a drunk; (or) an intoxicating substance/tonic  
**Ginsoak** \- Older English term for a drunk, à la 'wino' or 'rummy’ or ‘stewbum.’   
Gin-bathing (our usage) \- Drinking gin profusely, to the point of unconsciousness  
Splits of Champagne are a quarter of a size of a regular 750 mL bottle of Champagne. This means the bottle is 187 mL, which is equal to two small glasses or one large glass of Champagne. This might also be known as an "individual" or "single serving" bottle, and they often come in packs of four.  
Split of Champagne \- 1/4 size of a regular 750 mL bottle of Champagne. 187 mL: two small glasses or one large glass of Champagne. AKA "individual" or "single serving" bottle, often in 4-packs  
Papirosi \- AKA papirosa or papiroses \- Traditional Turkish and (more-recently Russian) unfiltered cigarettes. Wrapped in see through rice paper, an inch of strong tobacco is mated with a hollow cardboard tube. The cardboard looks like a giant filter, but it's hollow.  
Cheroot \- a cigar with both ends open and untapered.  
Panatela \- a long thin cigar  
Whiff \- a small cigar, open at both ends and about 3.5 inches long  
Cigaretto \- Alt. spelling of cigarette  
Shag \- fine-cut, loose tobacco. The name comes from its similar appearance to shag fabric  
Dottle \- the plug of burnt tobacco and ashes left in the bottom of a smoked pipe.  
**Bibativeness** (phrenology, obsolete) \- penchant for drinking, love of liquids (not necessarily alcoholic), thirst.  
*From an 1806 newspaper reader —* “\[I’ve heard of\] phlegm-cutter and fog driver\[,\] moistening the clay, of a fillip, a spur in the head, quenching a spark in the the throat, \[but\] never did I head of cock tail before.” — How’s Your Drink by Eric Felton (Fillip being a stimulus, or a flick)  
Venulated \- able to see the veins. common in the nose of advanced alcoholics  
[Drug slang terms](https://www.baxtercountysheriff.com/plugins/show_image.php?id=917)  
[Opium](https://www.mcieast.marines.mil/Portals/33/Documents/Safety/Abuse/Opium.pdf) \- can be a liquid, solid, or powder, but most poppy straw concentrate is available commercially as a fine brownish  
powder.  
**Slang for opium** \- Aunti, Aunti Emma, Big O, O, Black, Midnight Oil, Zero, Ah-pen-yen, Black pill, Chandoo, Chandu, Chinese Molasses, Chinese Tobacco, Dopium, Dover's Powder, Dream Gun, Dream Stick, Dreams, Easing Powder, Fi-do-nie, Gee, God's Medicine, Gondola, Goric, Great Tobacco, Hop/hops, Joy Plant, Mira (Spanish), O.P., Ope, Pen Yan, Pin Gon, Pox, Skee, Toxy, Toys, When-shee, Ze, Zero  
Morphine \- Miss Emma   
Dover's powder \- a traditional medicine against cold and fever developed by Thomas Dover. It is no longer in use in modern medicine, but may have been in use at least through the 1960s. A 1958 source describes Dover's Powder as follows: “Powder of Ipecacuanha and Opium.”  
Thomas Dover, M.D. (1660–1742) \- sometimes referred to as "**Doctor Quicksilver**", was an English physician. He is remembered for his common cold and fever medicine Dover's powder, his work with the poor in Bristol, and his privateering voyage alongside William Dampier and Woodes Rogers that rescued castaway Alexander Selkirk, the real-life inspiration for Robinson Crusoe.  
Cocottes (or coquettes) \- high class prostitutes (courtesans) in France during the Second Empire and the Belle Époque. They were also known as demi-mondes and grandes horizontales. Cocotte was originally a term of endearment for small children, but was used as a term for elegant prostitutes from the 1860s.

#### *The Forty Elephants*

Alice Diamond [‘Diamond Annie’](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=0QZEnyZyYo4&ab_channel=WeirdHistory) \= led the gang out of The Elephant and Castle Pub in Southwark (suh-thuhk).  
Southwark is a London borough that lies south of the Docklands, and on the other side of the Thames.  
They ‘looked like elephants’ laden-down with the goods they stole from merchants, hidden  under their crinoline hoop skirts.  
Shoplifting ring — they donned coats with hidden pockets and went to the west end.   
The leader was called the Queen of the Forty Thieves or Queen of the Forty Elephants (first was Mary Carr, and may have been the founder; once known as the most dangerous woman in London  
Hoister \- shoplifter  
They posed as maids and robbed families blind after earning their trust  
Diamond Annie grew up on the Elephants’ turf, and took leadership at 20 years old. Her sister was also a member. She covered her hands in diamonds, giving her a punch to beware of, and she was taller than the average London man at the time, 5’9”  
She once slipped stolen jewels into a detective’s pockets during an interrogation.  
High-end establishments had a blind spot for people (thieves) in haute couture.  
One woman wore a false arm to keep her other free for stashing items in her coat  
The crush — a group would swarm a counter asking to see jewellery, passing the item down the line until one of them tucked it away. The others made a ruckus for a distraction and proclaimed their innocence so the pilferer could flee undetected.  
Another ruse was to try on a necklace so that a decoy could be made, and then swapped out on the next visit. Diamond Annie herself was once used in a distraction-ploy once her reputation was known, drawing attention from security so another Elephant could make off with things.  
They thrived for decades, buying fast cars that could outrun the coppers.   
Marie Britten fell in love and got pregnant and wanted to leave the gang and after she got married, Annie rallied a mob of the other Elephants and wailed on her husband. In her 18 mos. in jail, Gillian Rose Kendall took over. Known as the bobbed-hair bandit, she used a smash and grab technique, even smashing cars into storefronts. Annie didn’t fight back for her position, instead running a cathouse in Lambeth until she passed away in 1952

#### *Recreation & Entertainment*

Cremorne Gardens \- Popular pleasure gardens by the side of the River Thames in Chelsea, London. They lay between Chelsea Harbour and the end of the King's Road and flourished between 1845 and 1877; today only a vestige survives, on the river at the southern end of Cheyne Walk. Cremorne is also a ward of the Royal Borough of Kensington and Chelsea.  
[Mazes](https://www.newyorker.com/magazine/2021/11/29/how-the-worlds-foremost-maze-maker-leads-people-astray) \-   
**Chanteuse** \- a female singer of popular songs, especially in a nightclub  
Schumannn and his [finger-stretcher](https://www.wqxr.org/story/weird-classical-when-schumann-ruined-his-fingers-and-his-concert-career/) \- “The conventional story is that Schumann, either impatient with his progress or just that committed to being the absolute best, devised a finger strengthening mechanism out of a cigar box. The machine would bend back one finger and keep it in place, while allowing the user to exercise the other digits. There may be some credence to this — finger strengthening devices weren’t uncommon in the 1830s, and several of them were available for home use. Unfortunately, it didn’t work. Schumann wound up partially paralysing his right index and middle fingers in the hope of making them stronger. But there is plenty of room for scepticism. In 1971, musicologist Eric Sams presented research that, if not entirely dismissing the “finger box” as myth, at least casts suspicion on why he was using it. See, Schumann may very well have been suffering from syphilis at this time. It’s a terrible disease that, in the 19th century, would have been treated with mercury, which is poisonous. And one of the symptoms of mercury poisoning is a paralysis that begins in the muscles of the fingers and works its way up the forearm. If this were the case, Schumann may not have been looking to strengthen his fingers out of short sighted expectations to get better, faster; but because the strength in those fingers had already started to go.”

#### 

#### *Idioms & Phrases*

**Give over** \- phrasal verb. If you tell someone to give over, you are telling them to stop doing something, usually because they are annoying you. \[informal\]  
Paint the town (red) \- informal. : to go out drinking, dancing, etc.  
Put it upon (oneself) / take it upon \- to give oneself the the task of (X)  
Stands to reason \- makes sense  
“Twig” \- means to catch on, so you don’t want an additional “on” or “onto”. You either **twig something**, or just twig, i.e. get wise — understand the statement or situation.  
In a trice \- in an instant, swiftly  
**Look at the state of you** \- A popular exclamation used in inner Dublin, "Look at the state o'you\!" implies that a person's attire, personal hygiene, intoxication level, or general demeanour is worrisome  
You forget your place \- watch your tongue/ you’re out of line  
Put an end to \- quit, stop  
**Bully for you**  \- good for you  
Your carriage is bad \- straighten up, that’s bad posture  
The rap \- the discussion (often at length) ‘so, that’s the rap on Einstein, if you ask me’ or ‘he’s been rapping about music for hours’ or ‘what’s the rap about?’ or ‘what’s the rap on Jim, then?’; it can also mean blame, as in ‘rap sheet’  
Bin someone \- to kill someone (also to throw something away)  
**Or some such** \- or something like that  
That’s as may be \- true enough, (but…)   
Comme il faut \- correct in behaviour or etiquette. "my friend is partial to cocaine—in every other way he is very proper and comme il faut" or “militarily comme il faut” (DFW)  
**Well nigh** \- almost  
Anyroad \- Anyway ‘Anyroad, I got the project back in working order.’  
Well done, you. \- Sweet way of saying good job  
Categorical \- it means absolute, unqualified, unconditional. If you ask someone to marry you and she says maybe, you might be able to persuade her. If it's no, you might still have a chance. But if she gives you a categorical no, she will never change her mind.  
In short order \- quickly  
Carry the can \- to accept blame or responsibility for something that one did not cause. “He made the error, but she was left to carry the can”  
**Dead as a (brass bolt)** \- very dead or long dead  
Set the table on a roar \- to cause a hilarious commotion of laughter  
Our objectives are in accord \- we have the same motivation  
This night \- (idiomatic) ‘tonight’  
Without peer \- unequalled; unrivalled; peerless. “All accounts agree, your skills are without peer.”  
All accounts agree \- by all accounts  
Despite all appearances \- although it might not look like it… (x) is…   
Mealy-mouthed \- "afraid to say what one really thinks," 1570s; first element perhaps from Old English milisc "sweet," from Proto-Germanic \*meduz "honey" (see mead (n.1)), which suits the sense, but if the Old English word did not survive long enough to be the source of this, perhaps the first element is from meal (n.2) on notion of the "softness" of ground flour (compare Middle English melishe (adj.) "friable, loose," used of soils).  
**A breach of taste** \- an abnormality in etiquette or formality   
I have no recourse (but to do X) \- I have no other choice (but to do X)  
Moral infidelity \- a breach of ethics  
Nothing but heartache \-   
**Happy-clappy** \- derogatory. adjective. of or denoting a form of evangelical Christianity in which members of the congregation sing and clap enthusiastically during acts of worship.  
Talking free-and-easy \- marked by informality and lack of constraint, or else, peaching  
Set to rights \- to put back in order “‘nothing a bite of food and a huckle-buff wouldn't set to rights.’”  
Lay it on brisk \- to hand out a beatdown  
At odds \- in a state of disagreement  
**You never** \- You didn’t… no way you did it that way… I don’t believe that’s the way you did it.  
Do yourself a mischief \- hurt someone/yourself. “‘put that gun away before you \[...\]’”  
Cannot do any credit \- can’t help you ‘that gun’s an item that cannot do a scholar any credit.’  
Shanks’ mare / shank’s mare \- your own two legs. “He was down to shank's mare.” (Scottish origin)  
Hedge off \- leave off / get out of here  
The sweated labour \- the working class (who “produce the riches of this city”)  
**Took (his/her) measure of** \- to assess or quickly give the once over  
By your leave \- with your permission  
At length \- after a while “At length he stopped, coughing bitterly.”  
**Have done with it** \- move on, be finished “I call it opportunity, and have done with it.”  
Heard tale (of) \- to hear tell of, to hear about  
**No two ways about it** \- no way around it  
S’truth \- Strike that’s truth / It’s true / It’s the truth  
Felo de se \- Latin for felony against oneself; suicide  
Of a sudden \- all of sudden  
Stripping off \- to strip naked  
Bugger it \- screw it  
My abilities are wanting \- lacking  
Into a very mizmaze\! \- into a tizzy  
Like sixty \-  “actually appeared in 1848 with no reference to speed, but rather meaning ‘with great force or vigour.’”  
With a whole heart \- wholeheartedly  
With even greater ease \- more easily  
Some few of which \- a few of which  
In earnest \- occurring to a greater extent than before  
Through and through \- entirely   
Nerved to the marrow \- thoroughly invigorated  
Call for \- demand “‘He had to hire women to do it, there was so much call for the job.’”  
It seemed \*an age since\* \- a long time  
Who “had it from” \- heard it from  
And sure \- for sure / to be sure  
Run rings \- run circles “you could run intellectual rings around anybody, but you don’t because you’re basically a very good person.”  
Aught to \- anything at all “don't think there could be *aught to* what that wicked gossip told to Jerry?”  
Tell against \- be a disadvantage “Miasma will tell against your health, lad,”  
That’s rather good \- that’s rich, nice one  
Wet the baby's head \- to have an alcoholic drink to celebrate the birth of a baby. “I had a few drinks on Sunday to wet the baby's head.”  
Sling your hook \- to go away, “finish your sandwich and sling your hook”  
To get rooked \- To get swindled; cheated

#### *Slang & Vernacular*

Particulars \- details  
Skint \- (of a person) having little or no money available. “He’s a writer, which means he’s skint.”  
Sell your lives, lads\! / Sell your life (dearly) \- Cause great injury or loss to an enemy or assailant before being killed. “the soldiers had sold their lives dearly, for a great many Germans had died too”  
Jack-tar or jack tar \- Sailors in the Royal or Merchant Navy. The name “Jack” was used generically to refer to a common man, in the way we might talk today about an average Joe. “Tar” referred to the tarpaulin or sailcloth, so the term “Jack Tar” distinguished a man from other Jacks.  
Wot \- that  “only one wot ever comes calling \[...\] lady wot was found today?”  
Fambly \- mispronunciation of family (from Lonely Hunter)  
Fahoodle \- nonsense (mine), and [others](https://www.mentalfloss.com/article/502300/19-old-timey-ways-call-bs)   
On the take \- to take bribes "he may be county sheriff, but he's on the take"  
Gadje \- used by Roma to mean all non-Roma \[also spelled Gadze or Gaje; (pejorative) “bumpkin,” “yokel,” or “barbarian”\]  
Act stupid \- play dumb  
Tinker \- a mischievous person, especially a playful, impish youngster; or, a gypsy  
Dead as mutton \- “dead as a doornail” (Dickens)  
Devil Dodger \- a preacher  
His nibs \- an important or self-important person — used in the phrases “his nibs or her nibs” as if a title of honour. Origin might have come from the slang term “my nabs,” meaning “my gentleman” or “myself.” “Nab,” according to the OED, refers to a head or a coxcomb (a fop or a dandy).  
**Coxcomb** \- a vain and conceited man; a dandy, a fop  
Deadhead or dead-headed \- Dull or stupid  
Flinty \- harsh and stoic. “her flinty stare” ‘flinty resolve.’  
Toff \- Rich or upper-class  
Scurrige \- 1\. To scurry/rummage at the same time; 2\. A hurly-burly, tumult, a free-for-all.  
Plummy \-  refined, upper-class (typically of an accent)  
Mudlark(s) \- 1\. a scavenger in river mud for valuables 2\. (historically) a street urchin.  
Jolterhead or jolter-head \- 1\. (archaic) a large or heavy head. 2 (now dialectal, chiefly England) a dunce, booby, blockhead, a stupid or foolish person  
Picket(s) \- a ruffian (comes from strikers/picketers)   
Breadbasket \- the stomach; or a region with heavy cereal production  
Kept her own counsel \- to say nothing about what one believes, knows, or plans. "she doubted what he said but kept her own counsel"  
Chaff \- banter “chaffing with the barman” \- D.E.  
Dead-flash \- Dashing; astonishing, stylish and impressive  
Vinegar-phiz \- Someone with a sour face and a disposition to match  
Bad hats \- Scoundrels; troublemakers  
Fraudster \- trickster / con man  
**Smirched** \- smeared, soiled  
Street urchin \- a child living mostly in the streets, especially in slum areas. Also, Guttersnipe  
Gamine \- (dated) a homeless girl who roams the streets. These days, it’s a young woman with a mischievous, boyish charm.  
Gobsmacked \- utterly astonished; astounded.  
Grizzling \- the action, typically by a child, of crying fretfully. “put an end to your useless, officious grizzling\!” \- D.E.  
Street-drab or drab \- a prostitute   
Fancy-jack \- a dandy or a blueblood, perhaps even a slur for homosexual (no source for that)  
Exiguous \- (formal) very small in size or amount.  
Unaccountably \- surprisingly/incredibly “Unaccountably, they seemed in the highest spirits.”  
Atomy / atomies \- (archaic) a skeleton or emaciated body  
Skullduggery \- underhanded or unscrupulous behaviour; trickery.  
Bullyrook \- A hectoring, boisterous fellow; a cowardly braggart; a bully. **Also written bully-rock.**  
Canny \- (Northern-English, Scottish) pleasant; nice. "she's a canny lass"  
Cripes \- Used euphemistically for Christ as a mild curse or expression of surprise. Criminy/crivvens  
Squire \- term of address: 1\. a country gentleman in England, esp the main landowner in a rural community. 2\. (informal, chiefly British) used by one man to another, esp, unless ironic, to a member of a higher social class.   
Goodman / goodmen \- a husband; a man not of gentle birth, used as a title; a master of a household  
Gentle (birth) \- meaning noble, of good breeding  
Cracksman \- a burglar, especially a safecracker.  
Humbug \- deceptive or false talk or behaviour. "his comments are sheer humbug"  
Humbugging \- deceit, trickery, or to act like a fraud or sham.  
**Peaching** \- To inform upon or tattle  
**Mum** \- Dialect spelling of ma’am, shortened from ‘madam’ which was formerly the ordinary respectful form of address to a woman   
Sapphic \- relating to lesbians or lesbianism  
Tardy \- meaning slow-witted  
Cove \- a man; British informal, dated.  
Towelling \- To physically beat someone very severely. A noun or pronoun can be used between "towel" and "up." Primarily heard in Australia. “...we gave 'em such a hell of a towelling that it knocked 'em into a panic.”  
**Spoony** \- In 19th-century British slang, spoon meant "simpleton (a meaning that may have been influenced by the "shallowness" of some spoons). That use of "spoon" brought about the adjective "spoony" to describe a silly or foolish person.  
Mortal \- very “‘They got mortal desperate…’”  
Turkey-Legs \- (made up?) maybe meaning soldiers, whose flared breeches (jodhpurs) look like them  
Phlegmatic \- calm, composed, even to the point of emotionlessness (good for its etymology)  
Redress \- remedy or set right (an undesirable or unfair situation) “redress the citizens’ grievances”  
Fleer / fleering \- to grin or laugh at; scoff; sneer. n. a derisory glance or grin. “a fleering grin”  
Dosshouse \- flophouse (offering space to sleep and simple communal amenities)  
**Swell** \- a person of wealth or high social position, typically perceived as fashionable or stylish. "a crowd of city swells"  
Sharp \- v. to cheat or swindle (someone), especially at cards. "the fellow is drunk, let's sharp him" n. A cheater.  
Creepah \- creeper/creepy person  
Like a china cup \- something that shatters or breaks easily.  
Street arab / street-arab \- a raggedly dressed homeless child wandering the streets.  
Nipper \- (chiefly British) 1\. boy employed as a helper (i.e. carter or hawker) 2\. Child, esp. a small boy  
**Foppish** \- concerned with one's clothes and appearance in an affected and excessive way (typically of a man). "he is foppish and vain" A Fop became a pejorative term for a foolish man excessively concerned with his appearance and clothes in 17th century England. Some of the many similar alternative terms are: **coxcomb**, fribble, **popinjay** or dandy, **fashion-monger**, and **ninny**.  
Twaddle \- 1\. Noun: trivial or foolish speech or writing; nonsense. "he dismissed the novel as self-indulgent twaddle" Similar: balderdash, blather, rubbish, tripe, hogwash, drivel, bilge, bosh, **guff**, piffle, poppycock, phooey, malarkey, dribble, codswallop, cack, codology, **flapdoodle,** bushwa, bunkum, tommyrot, gammon, toffee, bollocks, bulldust 2\. Verb ARCHAIC: to talk or write in a trivial or foolish way. "What is that old fellow twaddling about?"  
Guff \- To talk in a trivial way  
Flapdoodle \- trivial, foolish talk  
Onion-eater \- A man/woman that eats a courtesan’s rear end.  
Like sixty — you, or the person or thing to which you are referring — is going fast or doing something very quickly. “like sixty” actually appeared in 1848 with no reference to speed, but rather meaning “with great force or vigor.” “Like forty” was popular since the late 17th century, meaning “with great force.”  It appears that “like sixty” is just an inflated version of “like forty,” and didn’t, at least originally, have anything to do with speed.  
**Whelp** \- Noun; A puppy. Also, Verb \- (of a female dog) give birth to (a puppy). "Copper whelped seven puppies" Also \- in whelp: (of a female dog) pregnant. "busy time ahead if she’s in whelp"  
Knocker-up \- (Wiki) A knocker-up, sometimes known as a knocker-upper, was a profession in Britain and Ireland that started during and lasted well into the Industrial Revolution, when alarm clocks were neither cheap nor reliable. A knocker-up's job was to rouse sleeping people so they could get to work on time. By the 1940s and 1950s, this profession had died out, although it still continued in some pockets of industrial England until the early 1970s. The knocker-up used a baton or short, heavy stick to knock on the clients' doors or a long and light stick, often made of bamboo, to reach windows on higher floors.  
Behold / beheld \- to see “the most gorgeous and symmetrical clouds **I ever beheld**. Next morning, I looked eagerly at the weather, but all seemed tranquil; and whatever was being done **in the deep places of the sky**” (John Muir)  
Diddy \- small/little, or a breast ‘we don’t live like diddy guys\!’ \- Johnny Dogs  
Raff \- riff raff: rubbish, refuse; can be used to describe a manner of speaking (BS-heavy)  
Out of countenance \- Uneasy; ashamed, to cause to lose composure; embarrass; disconcert. “I'm sorry if I'm quiet—I made a big mistake at work today, and it really put me out of countenance.” Also; stare out of countenance. ‘He stared me out of countenance.’  
Straight-backwards \- wrong, incorrect “‘I should say you have that straight-backwards,’”  
I don’t (much) hold with \- To refuse to agree, go along, or comply with something.  
Pray, **don’t fash yourself** \- don’t trouble yourself about it… ‘fash,’ meaning worry or bother  
In camera \- a legal term that means in private. The same meaning is sometimes expressed in the English equivalent: in chambers. Generally, in-camera describes court cases, parts of it, or a process where the public and press are not allowed to observe the procedure or process. “In camera” is a Latin term which, in this context, can be understood to mean an "in private" session. These meetings are sometimes called "Board only" sessions.  
Don’t matter a hang \- it’s not much of a factor  
**You gave me a turn** \- (old-fashioned) frighten or shock somebody: You gave me quite a turn, creeping up on me like that\!  
Talking velvet \- silver-tonguing  
**Safe as houses** \- well-protected  
Informate \- to inform “perhaps you'll informate me concerning this grievance…”  
Alack \- an expression of dismay (archaic)  
Donk \- short for donkey (a fool)  
Noonday \- noon  
From whence (she) came \- where (she) came from  
Expired \- dead  
**Bawd** \- a woman in charge of a brothel.  
Flash house \-  A vacant house or apartment where criminals gather or make lodgings; a house frequented by flash people, as thieves and whores; hence, a brothel. Also, could be a ‘breeding ground for crime,’ where ladsmen and pickpockets live together, or other criminals gather/make lodgings.  
Facts of commerce \- (idiomatic) standard operating procedures for a business  
Rum’un / Rummun / Rum one \- ‘the adjective rum meaning strange, odd, different, peculiar etc. A Norfolk expression ‘Tha's a rummun ent it’ means 'It's a strange thing, isn't it'.’  
Morocco-bound \- Bound in morocco leather; (also figurative) solid, reliable, conservative.  
All about you \- everyone around you “‘When all about you are losing their heads…’”  
Bequest \- a gift  
Sure \- for sure “they’ll hear you sure.”  
Hard on his heels — just behind  
Ere \- before  
**Worse luck** \- used for saying that you wish a situation was different. “We’ve got maths this morning, worse luck.”  
**Under the rose** \- in confidence; under pledge of secrecy.  
Cakey \- I think in this context she means she isn’t too lily-white, naive, or stupid to see through him.   
Blarney \- talk that aims to charm, pleasantly flatter, or persuade. As in: "he had the “street charm” of an Irish politician, but this blarney concealed his inner self..." Also: V. To influence or persuade (someone) using charm and pleasant flattery.  
Po-faced \- humorless and disapproving. ‘Etymology probably from po, abbreviated slang based on French pot de chambre (“chamber pot”), after the distasteful expression anyone would adopt upon being presented with a full one.’  
Convenient \- a mistress, ‘caught in bed with his convenient’  
Right and fly \- well and good  
Patterer (British slang) \- 1\. a person who speaks glibly or rapidly. 2\. a person able to speak the jargon or lingo of a particular group. 3\. A street trader, esp. One who uses rapid, persuasive talk to attract customers; a salesperson  
Pasted \- as in ‘labeled’ or ‘glued.’  
Adventuress \- a woman who seeks social or financial advancement by dishonest or unscrupulous methods.  
Put no credit \- to distrust “‘I can put no credit in any of your counsel\!’”  
Gulling \- to fool or deceive someone  
A civil tongue \- a respectful and polite manner of speaking  
Cheap at the price \- a good deal  
Coshed \- whacked  
Dead flash \- very cool, hip  
‘On what authority?’  
Near as much \- almost  
**Stow it / Knife it** \- shut up “Stow it, and let me think\!”  
Peaceably \- peacefully  
Every appearance of \- Seemingly “He blew a cloud of foulness with every appearance of satisfaction.”  
Crazy as a bedbug \- nuts, or over-excited  
I stand a-ready \- I’m ready (for something extreme or challenging)  
No help for it \- no other solution  
For all the world \- seeming very much like “with quiet assurance, before the five jostling ruffians, *for all the world* like a school-teacher calming an unruly class”  
Do for him/them \- get them “‘do for him\! Swing'll choke you if we don't\!’ The **mufflered** man raised his voice. ‘Coppers here\!’ he screeched, like a man selling hot chestnuts. ‘Everybody, come up and do for these copper sons-of-bitches—’”  
Look sharp \- pay attention, be on your P’s and Q’s  
Inne \- Isn’t he? “‘A Yankee, inne?’”  
Shaite \- alt. spelling for shite  
Never in my dealings \- never in my experience (I’ve never encountered… X)  
**Gobless** \- Dazed, out of sorts  
Toe-rag or toerag \- A contemptible or despicable person.  
Bugger all \- Nothing “Bugger all to do but rehearse” (informal, rude)  
Shot through \- 1\. Burned out 2\. Containing a lot of some element “shot through with humour”

###### **Giving Thanks**

Much obliged, nice one, cheers, many thanks

###### **Insults & Interjections (Contemporary)**

Well, I’ll be a monkey’s uncle, blimey, bloody hell, Christ almighty, fancy that, get outta town, great Scott, I'll be I'll be damned, my goodness, butter my butt and call me a biscuit, **shut the chapel door**, stone me, stone the crows, strike me pink, what in God’s Heaven?, what do you know, you don't say, oh my days, Christ on a swiftwalker,  
I’m fucking mate \- I’m dead (as in checkmate?)    
Eyetie \- slur for an Italian, as in ‘eyetalian’  
**Billy-be-damned** (American, colloquial, 1849\) An expletive; accursed; damned. “He's a Billy-be-damned fool if he thinks that plan will work.” / “I would have walked right up to their fire just as big as billy-be-damned” The term indicates the Devil, following the imagery that pictured Satan as a monster in goat's form.

###### 

###### **Anachronistic Slang**

**Slubberdegullion** \- chiefly dialectal. : a dirty rascal : scoundrel, wretch.  
Fawner \- someone who humbles himself as a sign of respect; behaving as if he had no self-respect (apple polisher, bootlicker, groveler, groveller, truckler; type of: crawler, lackey, sycophant, toady).  
Callithump \- a noisy boisterous band or parade.  
Spanghew \- to throw violently into the air, esp. to throw (a frog) into the air from the end of a stick.  
Loblolly (insulting) \- originally a British word for "a thick gruel." Riffing on this, apparently, Americans later used the word to refer to an ugly, boggy mess.  
Blatherskite \- a person who talks foolishly at length; alteration of the Scottish compound blather skate (skate means "a contemptible person").  
**Hallanshaker** \- (chiefly Scottish) a wandering beggar.  
Rinsed \- beaten badly. Humiliated, ruined, destroyed. Rinse (v.) (Britain, slang) To thoroughly defeat in an argument, fight or other competition. \[or else to dog for capital, to take someone for all they got\]  
Beavering away \- to work in a very active and energetic way  
Up the swanny \- pregnant; or in a challenging or troublesome situation, especially one that cannot be easily resolved.

Bluffery \- Talk or behaviour designed to mislead

###### **To add to Production**

My born days \- EX: “In all my born days I was never so insulted.”  
Born a bit tired \- A chronically lazy person  
turn up like a bad penny \- \[British, old-fashioned\] to appear again in a place where you are not welcome or wanted ‘Pete goes down very well with everyone except Ross, who makes zero effort when he turns up like the proverbial bad penny.’

#### *Magic Lantern Shows*

Based on the Kinotropists in The Difference Engine — [Slide Archive](http://lucerna.exeter.ac.uk/slide/index.php?id=5061115)  
"Kinotrope" from DIFFERENCE ENGINE \- [SEE VIDEO](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Ea7fH9gFDAk)   
[The slides](https://www.theguardian.com/education/2020/sep/27/how-victorians-saw-the-plight-of-the-poor-through-a-moral-lens) – originally paintings, later prints or photographs – were displayed on transparent plates that were illuminated using an image projector known as a **magic lantern**.  
*The Whiskey Demon: or the Dream of the Reveller, a set of 12 slides made by Alfred Pumphrey of Birmingham in the late 1800s.*  
Church ministers, activists and charity workers would have shown the slides at church halls, or at orphanages and workhouses, Kember explained. But they would have also been shown to middle-class audiences at fundraising events.  
Magic lanterns waned in popularity as cinema took off.   
The manipulation of light to produce an image dates back millennia. In the late 18th century, showmen used lanterns to produce horror shows, or phantasmagoria. Terrifying images were produced, including ghosts projected onto smoke and shapes that moved on walls.  
In the 1880s and 1890s, more than 30 firms were engaged in the production of lanterns and slides in London alone. As the shows gained in popularity, they were set to music or the spoken word.

#### *Video Games* 

Primary influences were combat de-emphasized cRPG’s and steampunk games. Secondary influences include games with good stealth and puzzle mechanics, and horror games for atmosphere.

- Chummer is slang for a shadowrunner, or somebody running in the shadows of the Sprawl.  
- A Sprawl is a name for an extended cityscape, the result of generations of urban growth. Sprawls are the major centers of metahuman civilization, most of the world being reclaimed by nature or polluted beyond comfortable habitability.  
- Peripherals \- additional widgets for outfitting your deck.  
- “There's a lot of storage memory, I/O handlers and active memory in this bin. Without the MPCP, It’d just be a glorified commlink.”  
- I/O \- Input/Output \- Within the channel subsystem, the input/output processor (IOP) supervises the flow of data from shared main storage to I/O engines that, in essence, represent devices. The IOP does dynamic pathing (routing) to connect a device to an I-stream engine.

Craniometry \- Measurement of the cranium (the main part of the skull), usually the human cranium. It is a subset of cephalometry, measurement of the head, which in humans is a subset of anthropometry, measurement of the human body. In this case, it’s trying to suggest skull shape is tied to intelligence, wit, human potential, etc.

**Ferrotape** \- What’s inside OG cassette tapes (circa 1978\) ‘Iron(III) oxide or ferric oxide is the inorganic compound with the formula Fe2O3. Iron (III) oxide was the most common magnetic particle used in all types of magnetic storage, magnetic tapes and recording media. Cassette tapes are made of a polyester-type plastic film with a magnetic coating. The original magnetic material was based on gamma ferric oxide (Fe2O3).

##### Arcanum 

Pugilist \- a boxer, especially a professional one.  
“\[it\] went over like stone shoes at the swimming hole.”  
Top choices for characters: Muck Evil Eye (half-ogre bodyguard), Clarisse Vorak (student of Therapeutic/Anatomical disciplines, but lost the opportunity due to a reveal of her Half-Orc nature and being shunned for it), and Victoria Warrington (erstwhile lesser nobility, quick-witted gambler)  
Game starts on an Airship bound for the ‘New World’ — it crashes…   
‘Oh, blood and ashes\!’

##### Sanitarium

- “Hide & Seek” Level is particularly inspiring for the Orphanage Scenes near the end of Atticus’s story.

### **Literary References \-** 

Primary influences are Victorian-era fiction and historical accounts, as well as Steampunk novels. Writers like HG Wells, Charles Dickens, and Jules Verne are especially of note. It’s important that the fictional world is rooted in historical accounts, and that science fiction and magic elements should be based on actual scientific principles, while any magic is representative of mysticism and superstition at the time.

Books Include: League of Extraordinary Gentlemen, Dracula, Frankenstein, Sherlock Holmes, Dr. Who, To Say Nothing of the Dog, Invisible Man, Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde, Lady Mechanika, Twenty Thousand Leagues Under the Sea, various works by Charles Dickens, works by [R.F. Kuang](https://rfkuang.com/books/) such as The Poppy War, [A Night in the Lonesome October](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A_Night_in_the_Lonesome_October)

#### *Charles Booth’s London [Poverty Maps](https://booth.lse.ac.uk/map/15/-0.1200/51.5111/100/1) and Police Journals*

- Charles Booth wrote “Life and Labour of the People in London” — a multi-volume book surveying the lives and occupations of the working class of late 19th century London.  
- From a police notebook that was collected in Booth’s time — “North up Gravel Lane across bridge separating the East from the West basin of the \[St. Katherine\] London Docks. This and the other bridge in New Gravel Lane are called the Bridge of Sighs because of the numbers of suicides. A policeman is now stationed there between 3pm and 7am each day as a preventive. There has been a lull in the number the last 2 years but they (suicides) used to be very frequent.” — George H. Duckworth’s Notebook: Police and Publican District 7

“It’s a knowledge guild,” he said soberly. “The bosses, the big’uns, they can take all manner of things away from us. With their bloody laws and factories and courts and banks.… They can make the world to their pleasure, they can take away your home and kin and even the work you do.…” Mick shrugged angrily, his lean shoulders denting the heavy fabric of the greatcoat. “And even rob a hero’s daughter of her virtue, if I’m not too bold in speaking of it.” He pressed her hand against his sleeve, a hard, trapping grip. “But they can’t ever take what you know, now can they, Sybil? They can’t ever take that.” (19)  
“Kinotrope” \- original vernacular meaning \- a display of wooden widgets of undescribed dimensions that by means of the machine rotated or were swapped out to act as pixels and display images and text. They are used in The Difference Engine in place of/in conjunction with stage theater, public lecture (i.e. powerpoint) and as a means of advertisement and news reports. The pieces of wood made a distinct clacking noise as they transitioned from one position to another lending the programmers that operated them the title "clackers".

“passed for domesticity” (21)

**\[RAT BAITING BAR\]**  
“a public house known as the Blue Boar. ‘A SPORTING-GENTLEMAN,’ he was informed by a large bill, ‘a Staunch Supporter of the destruction of these Vermin,’ would give ‘A GOLD REPEATER-WATCH TO BE KILLED FOR BY DOGS under 13 3/4 pounds weight.’ Below the smudged bill, a painted wooden placard advertised ‘Rats always on hand for the accommodation of Gentlemen to try their dogs.’ He entered, and shortly was greeting Fraser in the rank smell of dogs, tobacco-smoke, and **hot** **penny-gin**. The long bar was crowded with **men of every grade of society**, many with their animals under their arms. "There were bull-dogs, Skye terriers, small brown English terriers. The room was low-ceilinged and quite unadorned. About the walls were hung clusters of leather collars.” 353

But she was smiling, and it happened rarely enough that I was happy to smile back, and think about the future as an even road we would all drive along together, and make out all right.”

#### *Poetry*

##### *\[Buffalo Bill 's\] by E.E. Cummings*

Buffalo Bill ’s  
defunct  
               who used to  
               ride a watersmooth-silver  
                                                                  stallion  
and break onetwothreefourfive pigeonsjustlikethat  
                                                                                                     Jesus

he was a handsome man   
                                                  and what i want to know is  
how do you like your blue-eyed boy  
Mister Death

##### *The Bridge of Sighs by Thomas Hood (1799–1845)*

*Make no deep scrutiny*

*Into her mutiny*

*Rash and undutiful:*

*Past all dishonour,*

*Death has left on her*

*Only the beautiful.*

Several clues in the poem, which harps upon beauty, sins and scorn, hint that the woman was pregnant and had been thrown out of her home.

*Sisterly, brotherly,*

*Fatherly, motherly*

  *Feelings had changed:*

*Love, by harsh evidence,*

*Thrown from its eminence;*

*Even God's providence*

  *Seeming estranged.*

### **Miscellaneous Notes \-** 

#### 

#### *Dialogue Programming & Testing*

- To Find Latest Stats \- Top menu of Unity, select Adventure Creator-\>Editors-\>Game Editor. In the popup window select the Variables tab, and there's a big list of variables that you can scroll through, which includes all the skills and their initial values.  
- Current Stats — Brute Force, 28, Protection, 22, Intimidation, 26, Combativeness, 20, Protection, 22, Impulse, 14, Recollection, 25, Deduction, 20, Persuasion, 18, Chemistry, 12, Mechanisms, 13, Fine-Motor, 20, Reflexes, 13, Hand-Eye, 17, Haste, 18, Stealth, 12, Discretion, 13, Conjuring, 30, Composure, 23, Diligence, 16, Constitution, 26, Vision, 10, Touch, 20, Hearing, 15, Smell, 25, Taste, 22, Blood, \-3, Phlegm, 4, Black Bile, 3, Yellow Bile, \-2  
- For AC-Delays, try the @ sign after a command to delay it by some number of seconds. So something like… AC(AutomatonWalk)@2.5  
- When using the @X functionality to delay actions, if that node also has a Continue(), we need to delay the Continue as well. If you delay action 1 and then say Continue(), we move to the next node and action 1 never happens. If ‘continue’ is tied for the longest delay and it's also listed last, it will happen last.  
- \-Hold "P" when selecting a skill check to pass regardless of card drawn.  
- \-Hold "F" when selecting a skill check to fail regardless of card drawn.  
- \-Hold "M" after drawing a non-face card to draw a major arcana card anyway.  
- Atticus’s starting attire — Magician’s topcoat

#### 

#### *Various Reminders*

- Think ‘bidirectional’ with dialog trees, considering options for both Human and Minotaur forms.  
- EXT. RED LANTERN LANE \- NIGHT \-  (Add this area in later as one the Player can return to — will include the Chinese Laundry and the Red Lotus)  
- Jump from the Bridge of Sighs (if desired, for Atticus, or Clara, or both?)  
- Atticus has a ‘Bookmaker’ not a Bookie; distances should be in km  
- **Links to revisit \-** [1](http://www.michaelyoungkin.com/health-and-sanitary-conditions-in-london/#:~:text=During%20the%20Victorian%20Era%20in,Scarlet%20fever%2C%20Cholera%2C%20etc.&text=One%20of%20the%20biggest%20sanitary,horses%20and%20use%20of%20cesspools), [2](https://www.bl.uk/victorian-britain/articles/health-and-hygiene-in-the-19th-century), [3](https://londonist.com/2016/07/where-did-trafalgar-square-s-pigeons-come-from),  
- Sovereign Syndicate was a company that was ‘wound up’ in 1896\. Charles Selby was the Liquidator  
  The fancy (peculiar colored) rats were kept as pets by many members of high society, who organised various competitions, promoting the hobby well into the 20th century.  
- Cockney accent: glottal ‘T’ wa’er not ‘water.’ The ‘l’ that sounds like a ‘w’ — ‘piw’ not ‘pill’ and the schwa sound: Dawctah not ‘doctor.’  
- The night in the Brother Bedroom with Clara is less about sex, and more about desire for comfort and the personal, intimate connection that Atticus scarcely receives.   
- [Assassin's Creed Steampunk-style concept art/posters/billfolds/broadsides](https://www.yeaaah-studio.com/assassins-creed-london-gangs)  
- Lori / Heather / Tresa / Amanda can be consulted on Tarot and Astrology if needed.  
- Atticus Awakens is set in the Fall because of the Harvest Moon (Werewolves) and Halloween (for witch-doctors and Magick).  
- Atticus is a bird that has come to like his cage — even as he sings about how he wishes to escape his vices, he actually likes them, and the way they keep him chained.  
- Last of Us Part II [concept art](https://www.artstation.com/artwork/188Z0L)
