#### *The Masked Stranger*

##### *His Motivations — Slowly revealed the Player:*

- The stranger sought you out because not many Minotaurs can do magic anymore. The magical bloodlines have died off, for a reason we find out by the end of the story. The more observant players will be able to put together a strong hypothesis near the final mission, but it cannot be confirmed until the ‘Big Reveal.’  
- He puts you under hypnosis while you’re in the opium dream.  
- Since society hasn’t not been accepting Minotaurs, some who embrace the darkness of being lonely, or are forced into such a life like Atticus, develop magic as a psychic coping mechanism. Their brain chemistry opens in certain pathways that ‘well-adjusted’ Minotaurs don’t develop. They need to be in considerable isolation to develop magical abilities. Like Atticus did in the orphanage having suffered bullying, and much like the legendary Greek Minoatar did, confined to his Labyrinth. Since there isn’t a mainstream culture for this sort of isolated-upbringing, that’s why there aren’t many magical Minotaurs left.   
- Besides, the high-class minotaur families that still exist would rather raise their children and be a part of their lives, teaching them the simple illusion of making them look human, rather than raise them in isolation, even if it would make them more magically powerful. There are of course exceptions to the rule, and Atticus has heard of the famous magical Minotaur families. But the stranger has to tell him about why they became so magical. And why this was dark magic, because magic in isolation typically leads to darkness, which is also why society has shunned minotaurs. They don’t count the cost, and the more sociopathic they are, the more powerful they tended to be throughout history. Which made them powerful allies in the world of crime, but now that most crime has gone underground and white collar, and big governments have cracked down, the minotaur crime-lords have long since been killed off by the other races. In a similar way to how the dragons were slayed to prevent a reign of terror.

The proprietor says, “This is a process only for the preservation of Human bodies. Automatons are strictly forbidden from tainting such a time-honoured tradition.”

#### *Atticus Daley*

- Ungulates \-  members of a diverse clade of primarily large mammals with hooves. These include odd-toed ungulates such as horses, rhinoceroses and tapirs, and even-toed ungulates such as cattle, pigs, giraffes, camels, sheep, deer, and hippopotamuses. M*embers of a diverse clade of primarily large mammals with hooves. These include odd-toed ungulates such as horses, rhinoceroses and tapirs, and even-toed ungulates such as cattle, pigs, giraffes, camels, sheep, deer, and hippopotamuses.*  
- *Clade \- a group of organisms believed to have evolved from a common ancestor, according to the principles of cladistics.*  
- Those who don't care for him may call him an ungulate. We can also call him a Chordate because he has a tail.  
- Our Minotaurs use horns as close range weapons, they’re light sensitive and have an innate sense of direction. Size and strength above average vs. human.  
- Christina \- A separate love interest, giving Atticus personal interest in the case, rather than Clara's more general motive; also the dead body that Atticus finds in

#### *Sister Susanna Haverford*

- Used to be Eleanor Everdeen / Elford, or Susanna Partridge; see [Supporting Doc](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1m_UrwJEknvjy53HJSvydu3EdO5AjJjdOqZt5ZF9o740/edit?usp=sharing)  
- After so many years of Father Franco's physical (we've decided to avoid any mention of sexual abuse) abuse of the children, bad spirits have found their way into the orphanage, and Everdeen is sort of a perpetually scared, worn-out but good-at-heart character. She's paranoid having dealt with Father Franco, who has since moved away from the orphanage and into a higher position in the Clergy, leaving Everdeen to manage the orphanage. The numbers of orphans have dwindled, partly due to other orphanages being built in the area, but a majority due to Father Franco being in cahoots with this Cult that's obsessed with steampunk-style body-modification and eternal-life chasing, by way of organ-harvesting and occult experiments/borderline witchcraft. They are harvesting organs from the orphans, since they have no family and are easily prey, and Franco, along with the Dockhounds (most powerful gang in the East End) are involved in the organ-market, since the upper class paying top dollar for these organs (used for weird operations and human enhancements, as well as actual organ replacements for rich people with bad livers, etc.) The leader of the Cult is the principal antagonist for the whole Sovereign Syndicate (the team that the M.S. assembles \-- Teddy, Clara, included), and the Cult is trying to usurp the Crown's power by way of infiltrating the Christian church. So... Everdeen is short-tempered and her nerves are worn thin. She's seen a lot of tragedy with her orphans disappearing, and suspecting Franco but also not really knowing how to confront him, because she fears for her own life, and she knows about the Cult's influence, but will refuse to discuss it with the Player. The Cult has a kind 'Eyes Wide Shut' feel to it, the Cult leader having been crippled in his youth, but inheriting a boatload of money, which he then used smartly to build more wealth, and he rides around in a tricked-out steampunk wheelchair with all these mechanical augmentations and weaponry. Think of Susanna as Jessica Lange in AHS Asylum... I'm super open to changing her character, too, so maybe she's in with Franco on the orphan harvesting, like maybe in a Walter-White sort of way, *she breaks bad because she needs a liver* or something, and there was this one terrible orphan (maybe a character like Jimmy Marker, Atticus's tormentor) who she just got fed up with and sold to the Cult so she could get his organ. But IDK that could be going too far.  
- \*Crown being the British Crown and the real (historically) Parliament / British Govt.

#### *Eustace Fullivole*

##### *Cragside Notes (Reference for the “Fullivole” Manor)*

- The Manor lies and was built “on the side of a moorland crag.”  
- Shooting box \- chiefly British: cabin/small house in the country for use in the shooting season.  
- He hired a Scottish architect called R. Norman Shaw, \[and\] Cragside became the centre of Armstrong's world; reminiscing years later, in his old age, he remarked, "had there been no Cragside, I shouldn't be talking to you today – for it has been my very life"  
- Shaw sketched out the whole design for the "**future fairy palace**" in a single afternoon, while Armstrong and his guests were out on a shooting party. After this rapid initial design, Shaw worked on building the house for over 20 years. The long building period, and Armstrong's **piecemeal, and changeable,** approach to the development of the house, and his desire to retain the original shooting lodge at its core, occasionally led to tensions between client and architect, and to a building that lacks an overall unity.  
- Shaw made sure Cragside was composed "with memorable force"  
- In August 1884 the Prince and Princess of Wales (the future Edward VII and Queen Alexandra) made a three-day visit to Cragside; it was the peak of Armstrong's social career. **The royal arrival at the house was illuminated by ten thousand lamps and a vast array of Chinese lanterns hung in the trees on the estate; fireworks were launched from six balloons, and a great bonfire was lit on the Simonside Hills.**  
- \[In 1887\] at the Golden Jubilee of Queen Victoria, Armstrong was ennobled as Baron Armstrong of Cragside, and became the first engineer and the first scientist to be granted a peerage. Armstrong died at Cragside on 27 December 1900, aged 90, and was buried beside his wife in the churchyard at Rothbury.  
- Located in [Ottershaw](https://www.google.com/maps/place/Ottershaw,+UK/@51.3814073,-0.5563589,11.8z/data=!4m12!1m6!3m5!1s0x487dfb409284ffd1:0x29283750cdaba67f!2sNational+Trust+-+Cragside!8m2!3d55.3173625!4d-1.8939049!3m4!1s0x487677a538e09d8f:0x5fadfe5a6be0f3f7!8m2!3d51.3633942!4d-0.5303049)?

##### *Craigdarroch Castle in Canada  \- [A comprehensive Tour](http://tour.thecastle.ca/tour)*

- Isaac’s memory \- “outside the downtown core on a large plot of land (something like 28 acres originally it says) up on a hill with great views overlooking the city. Large gardens, outbuildings for maids quarters, support staff, gardener, etc. really intricate wood and stone work, stained glass windows”  
- [It’s haunted](https://npiweb.com/Blog/Posts/haunted-places-series-craigdarroch-castle). [Probably](https://mysterioustrip.com/craigdarroch-castle-victoria/)…  
- Based on Craigdarroch castle. Built by coal baron \[Vancouver Island coal\] Robert Dunsmuir during the reign of Queen Victoria, \[...\] Robert and Joan Dunsmuir had two sons, eight daughters and plenty of exploits.   
- Victorian society was highly stratified into upper, lower and middle classes. Craigdarroch was home to individuals whose great wealth was predicated on the efforts of the working class, but also the workplace of a number of servants. The Castle was designed to keep these two spheres separate, and yet close enough that the servants were always on hand to assist the family.

#### *The [Ratcatcher](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1dfUzZTXY6pG3hJjKmA0Qb7MIzEIlPKZfKE5WH6_OvgE/edit)*

- “...Jack Black became synonymous with the profession of a [ratcatcher](https://www.thevintagenews.com/2017/11/09/ratcatchers-victorian-age/#:~:text=The%20people%20involved%20in%20this,sometimes%20using%20traps%20or%20dogs.). But he wasn’t only known for his ability to efficiently kill rats. Black was the pioneer of a fad that became popular across Victorian England in the mid-19th century―**the fancy rat. In his ventures, Black would always be on \[the\] lookout for peculiar colored rats**, which he would then sell on as pets. The culture of breeding rats was, at the time, related to rat-baiting―a blood sport in which a group of rats was released in a pit, together with a dog, who would then chase them, and eventually kill them”  
- The pastime of [rat-baiting](https://www.ozy.com/true-and-stories/the-other-jack-black-and-his-rat-friends/250037/) eventually became so lucrative that the London government put a tax on rat-killing dogs.  
- The ratcatcher has a little cellar in Sorris Square which is his home and office, with an entrance to the Underground locomotive-tunnels.

#### *The Stray Dog (Sorris Square)*

- Blue merle collies were originally known as tortoise-shell to describe their colour.  
- Queen Victoria’s deep and abiding love for Scotland made a lasting impact on the Collie’s history. It was the dog-loving Victoria, during her many extended stays at Balmoral Castle on the Scottish Highlands, who popularised the local herding breed among her courtiers and subjects during the second half of the 19th century. Her enthusiasm for Collies began the breed’s ascent from humble shepherd dog to worldwide canine superstar.  
- It is supposed that the Collie’s ancestors reached Scotland nearly 2,000 years before Victoria did, brought by the Romans during their conquest of Britain in the first century of the Common Era. Over several centuries, the Roman herding stock was interbred with local dogs. Sometime during this long unwritten history, we can surmise that a stouthearted sheepherding dog recognizable as the Collie came into focus. (A prevalent theory holds that the name Collie derives from the name of a particular strain of black-faced sheep called colleys.)  
- The Collie enters the written record around 1800, and by the time Victoria “discovered” the Collie later in the century, the breed’s now familiar characteristics were set. The Collie was a working dog breed developed in Great Britain, probably by the 18th century. There are two varieties: the rough-coated, originally used to guard and herd sheep, and the smooth-coated, used mainly to drive livestock to market. Collies are lithe dogs with tapering heads, almond-shaped eyes, and erect ears that tip forward at the ends. Both varieties are identical in form, although some authorities believe they were once distinct breeds. The rough-coated collie, a popular companion and watchdog, has a thick, straight coat that forms a heavy ruff about its neck and throat; the smooth-coated collie has a dense, smooth coat.  
- Border collie, breed of herding dog, typically an outstanding sheepdog, which has been used along the English-Scottish border for about 300 years. Considered among the most intelligent breeds, border collies also excel at agility competitions.  
- Bearded collie, a dog breed developed in Great Britain for herding sheep and driving cattle to market, is one the oldest British breeds, and its ancestors may have included herding dogs from the Continent. The bearded collie is a medium-sized dog covered with a long, shaggy coat covering even the hanging ears, tail, legs, and muzzle (hence, bearded); its long eyebrows blend into the coat on the head.

#### *Character Etiquettes, Skills and Tags*

- Potential Tags — Street urchin (someone living or making their living mostly in the streets), drab / courtesan, gin-bibber (drunkard), flunky (servile Dockhound), workman, hawker (anyone selling bulk fruit, veggies, fish, or other goods, mostly in Sorris Square), goodman (not of noble birth), sycophant, noble (as in of royal blood or status), gentry (of good social position, but below nobility), well-heeled (wealthy), plummy (snooty, upper class), vinegar-phiz (short-tempered, could go along with the Volatile tag), jolterhead / pillock (a foolish person), bullyrook (boisterous but cowardly, a bully), starveling (malnourished), fraudster / bad hat (scoundrel, troublemaker), scapegrace (a ne'er-do-well) , penny-boy or penny-girl (low wage errand-type worker), luddite, card-sharp (for the gambling parlor), vagabond, bobby or copper (police), top brass (a higher-up in the police force), scholar, antiquarian (such as the Baron, or anyone with a private historical collection fo some kind), blackguard (criminal, or generally immoral/dishonorable person), engineer, cabinet man / cabinet woman (works for a politician), debonair (think Masked Stranger or any dandy/genteel NPC), fancy-jack (a dandy or blueblood), canny (pleasant, agreeable), adventurer / adventuress (well-traveled, like Anselina, or the Baron)

##### *Skills / Skill Set / Points / Attributes / Affiliation Notes*

- Patience AKA Understanding of Human & Worldly Flaws (willing to wait for a problem to resolve itself) \[for OG stats — equivalent to Self Discipline\]  
- Insight AKA Empathy (predicting what characters will say if you say ‘this’)  
- Charity AKA Compassion (willingness to help others, this helps b/c ‘what goes around comes around’)  
- Persuasion (talk your way through, lying if necessary)  
- Integrity AKA Honesty (makes you seem reliable to NPCs)  
- Sleight of Hand (lock picking, dexterity, deftness with small tools/games)

### **Notes from Youtube (Instructional & Inspirational Videos) \-** 

#### [*Choice*](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=-FfITxaXeqM)*, Complicity, and Consequence*

- Choice, obvious, complicity — the emotional experience during a choice, and consequence, obvious  
- Many types of consequences, such as narrative, gameplay and deferred (Failbetter likes to warn players about these, but not all studios do)  
- Write choices that align with, comment on, unpack the Theme  
- If you can express/render down a ton of backstory in a way that’s immediately emotionally engaging, then that’s a good Choice  
- Ludonarrative dissonance \- the conflict between a video game's narrative told through the story and the narrative told through the gameplay. Ludonarrative, a compound of ludology and narrative, refers to the intersection in a video game of ludic elements and narrative elements. Ludic meaning ‘of play,’ and meaning of the gameplay, here.  
- 2, 3, 5 Choices: 2 \- to be or not to be (dramatic, elegant); 3 \- left, right, straight (the third choice comments on the first two); 5 is better than 4 (it feels generous, and you can lock or two of them with variables, with making Player feel restricted)  
- Don’t write yourself too many Content Checks that you can’t pay.  
- In Fallen London, you get 47 justifications for how entered a selective Member’s Club  
- Some argue that choices shown, but unavailable takes you out of Immersion  
- Dictating a Player’s emotional state is a **missed opportunity**. Try not to do this so much/cut it down in the rewrite process with Scott.

![][image4]

- If you replace the ‘be humbled’ prompt with a ‘take action’ prompt that’s related to the corresponding set of feelings, you add colour, and the player already knows how they feel when they make the choice, you don't have to spell it out  
- You can have your body hollowed out after joining a cult, but it makes no gameplay difference. This creates a ‘fantasy of failure’ which people love.  
- The game is what happens inside your player’s head, not what happens in the code or script. If the player doesn’t notice a consequence, it doesn’t happen. Don’t just spray choice and consequence all over, even as an indie dev full of piss and vinegar. **The more choice/consequence you have in your game, the less players care about any given one.** Quantity/Quality  
- “A \[title\] of your quality (dictated by variable)  
- Find the common factors in your story — “the stokers smoulder” every time there’s combat, this is repeatable yet continuously evocative  
- **Underspecified narrative** — START USING THIS TERM. It’s when you leave space for the players to interpret/insert their own thoughts and feelings, so they co-own the narrative for much greater attachment. Write as much as you need to get in, then get out of the way  
- “...it is with words as with sunbeams \- the more they are condensed the deeper they burn.” — Southey  
- “Whatever you can communicate sufficiently in game mechanic, you should put it there instead of in text.” — Emily Short  
- Resist the urge to solve a design flaw with text. Players don’t pay you by word. Game mechanics are a way to allow the player to cooperate with you.   
- Resources should be dependent on the theme, even if it’s just the amount of time spent playing. Don’t have dozens, and have the few carry symbolic/emotional weight  
- Make it appear that the game is *paying attention* to the player.  
- Construct an outline that’s like fires in the desert, and the paths the player navigates between the points will belong to them.

#### 

#### [*Everything's on Fire*](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=POgN1VN3e3k) *and No One Knows What to Do (In Game Writing)*

- 3 min — What we do we can only do with the help of the Programmers, the Artists, with the help of QA. I love telling stories, and I want everyone to feel the same way. It’s disheartening to hear when someone says ‘eh, it’s a paycheck.’ … Oh… but this is my baby :)  
- If they are not involved, then we might as well be writing novels.  
- 4:49 Game writing is a bridge between what the designers intended and what stories the Players are telling themselves. (The Designers have an overarching idea of what’s possible, the writers get to lead the Player through what story they themselves (personal QOL) along the way.  
- Emerging narrative \- 50% of the story is being told in the Player’s head. It’s a more personal story than the Writer can tell (because we’re writing for a wider audience)  
- The Player is telling the story with you  
- A narrative bug is a very real thing  
- Story vs. Setting (which would be there if the Player didn’t exist)  
- Just because you have one, doesn’t mean you have the other  
- When you’re down in the thick of writing, you’re just seeing tree, tree, tree, and it’s hard to see the forest.  
- Artists will provide insights into characters that we never knew we were lacking.  
- Pick your battles — what’s crucial? Also, don’t shut anyone down, because they might not come back to you with a new idea later on.  
- Watch out for your ego, when you’ve worked on something super hard and it still doesn’t resonate  
- Rule of Three — it’s okay if one person critiques something, but when you’ve gotten the same note from three people, then there’s a real problem  
- Think of everything you’re writing as incomplete — the Story is a sculpture.  
- Near completion, don’t hesitate to push back, but do it in the language of the Producers.  
- It’s important to ask *why* things are changing… bring in a Focus Test to check and see if the creators are just getting weary of their own content, and hence forcing an unnecessary amount of changes.  
- Write like a Designer and vice-versa: **Floating NPC’s** — Can be used interchangeably and moved levels without compromising the integrity of the Quest. It will still work just as well, whether NPCx is in this level or the next one.   
- Narrative vs. Level-flow — Can you remove the Story, write the Level-flow first, including the major beats, then fill in the gaps.  
- Create a Critical Path document (complete with floating NPC’s), because the chances are that the game will get re-scoped. USE A FLOW CHART  
- Ambition is quite often the enemy of reality  
- Re-pacing and re-scoping is an important part of the process towards the end, b/c let’s face it, we’re writers, and we tend to write long.  
- Make a list of all the Gameplay Spaces we’ve already created (and can be slightly adjusted and still used to add to the game) EX: ‘Rome’ becomes ‘Rome at night’  
- What parts of the Story are important to be delivered and WHEN?  
- Expose the Questions\! Otherwise the writer is just asking them to themselves… (This \*should\* give Questioner-type Personalities something of an advantage to lean into, if moderated well)  
- Follow the suboptimal paths through your dialogues — not just the one you would choose and would make the most sense.   
- Enjoy fixing bugs — like little crunchy treats at the end — feels good to smash-em. Don’t be afraid to say ‘Will not Fix’ or ‘Working as Intended’  
- If Players are skipping Dialog, try to build in Tutorialization throughout the game to reinforce the points you were trying to hit.  
- Lean on the Journal UI (so anything critical that came in dialog is replicated there, too)  
- Get creative with tutorialization — reiterate learned skills/how to use items via Gameplay and Visuals (a la the Half-Life 2 Silent Tutorial)  
- Make sure the choices you offer have a lot of reactivity — so with a limited budget, you might only be able to have 4 or so ‘Big Choices,’ but for those Big Choices, pay the heck back on them… make sure you milk them for all the reactivity they have to offer. And draw attention to the strategic Choice-Branching you designed and wrote for. You can literally say — because you killed that guy, that’s why I’m coming after you now. Sometimes drawing attention to the choice multiple times will make sure more Players realise the amount of control they actually did have, and the results their choices brought about.   
- Some branching choices will move toward ‘Choke-Points’ — big moments that ALL Players will get to, and thereby will be more useful to write. They will make it through the edits, because they hold up the storyline.  
- Can you make the Reactivity systemic and yet still narrative based — so for example, no matter what Dialog choice you make, it affects your character’s Affiliation  
- Write a summary of the game, then write the game, then see if it still matches the initial summary… but I’m not sure about this advice, because I like the option to change things.

#### [*Writing ‘Nothing’*](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=LPkbAMj-xVA)

- Keep the compression while adding complexity  
- The Player should be Actively Listening (so try adding more subtext — internal questions the characters might be asking themselves, or lies they’re telling themselves)  
- One type — a ‘mechanics-driven’ game  
- Omissions:   
  - Use contrast to express the inexpressible  
  - As you start practising this (don’t forget to add ellipses for the actors)  
  - EX: Do you think we’ll make it through to ‘till dawn?  
  - ~~I don’t know, but we’ll try.~~  
  - I… We’ll try.  
- Apparent Non-sequiturs:  
  - EX: Do you think we’ll make it through to ‘till dawn?  
  - I… \[Subtext — silence covering her fear\] Don’t forget your coat, it looks like rain in the morning.  
  - Even in games about shooting Zombies, there’s still some love (in) there.  
- Unreliable Narrators:  
  - Add some uncertainty to etch-out the priorities of the characters, but don’t overuse them, because they can seem like ‘Clues’ but are Red Herrings  
  - Self-deception (when obvious to the Player) will allow the Player to appreciate the impending Self-Discovery — relevant to Atticus’s story  
- Go Beyond Script — Animation, Voice Acting, Environmental (Art Design), Interactables and Interfacing, Marketing (give clues about the Story outside the Game itself)

#### [*Side Quests*](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=yTJ_RjfhGVQ)

- Convenient to accept and woven into main story; letting player choose what they want to see makes more players satisfied in the end  
- Types — story driven, variety driven, elaboration driven, and challenge driven  
- Story — attached to Main Quest, but not necessary to finish the game  
- Variety — adds weirdness and flair; a palate cleanser  
- Challenge-driven — such as optional ‘super-boss’ battles

#### 

#### *Quest Design ([1](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=otAkP5VjIv8) & [2](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ur6GQp5mCYs))*

- Common types — kill, fetch, fed-ex, collect, escort (with NPC)  
- Most are used to direct the Player through the experience (teach you how to play, mechanics, etc.)  
- Make them story-driven and advantageous to the Player in later acts (if: found Orphans, then Molly is impressed at the Tavern, for example, or if: CourtesanKiller, then Clara doesn’t rob you *entirely* blind)  
- Ex: follow the sewer caps — suddenly this part of the world that the Player has run over many times are part of the lore of the world; then a puzzle makes them think of places on the map under new terms, such as the Town Hall being the ‘seat of power’  
- The sewer caps have to be textured either way. What's the extra cost of making them shine a bit?  
- Environmental quests — such as Missing Poster, make the world feel holistic and alive

#### *Quests and Rewards*

- Overjustification effect \- when offering a reward ends up reducing the intrinsic motivation to perform an action. (such as test subjects that already like drawing being less motivated to do so when they are given a reward for it, compared to a group that was not given a reward) The journey is the destination, the reward is in the process, etc., like when I began enjoying film work as I started to associated it more with payment  
- However, the Unexpected Reward avoids falling victim to this effect. This group of subjects drew slightly more/better pictures than the group receiving no reward at all, so GIVE PLAYERS UNEXPECTED SURPRISE REWARDS  
- Better to use large, overarching goals that players can complete however they want, rather than restrictive, step by step instructions (allowing design to focus on comparative metrics like leaderboards, histograms, and personal bests)

#### *Sidequests*

- Types: Kill, Delivery, Fetch, Escort (protect someone), Collection (like our Gin Collector), Investigation, Defensive (plants vs. zombies), Gather, Hybrid, Push the Button, Mystery, Lore

  #### *Do [Good Work](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=4DWdnoLosZ8) When You Don't Know What to Do*

- Ruth Tomandl — love/hate with ambiguity: when you don’t have enough info to make good decisions; this is not risk, which is when you bet on something but at least you know the odds  
- Game Devs face ambiguity ALL the time in decisions; if you’re doing something NEW, then you should obviously be facing ambiguity  
- Getting the right work done (prioritisation, driving decisions, scheduling and tasking, owning deadlines); seek a viable, compelling, clear and well communicated shared vision.   
- Issues — churn without progress, feature creep, team anxiety, resentment/blame and indecision  
- 14:55
